```
Ken wrote:

<<snip technical details>> 

> Executed some grep one-liners until I put the debugged results into a
> shell script (used temp files rather than piping to help debug
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> the industry.

That would be a difficult answer to your question. But I might try. You
can concatenate a lot of lines with sed (before your grep), where the
last line of a serie should have a single dot for termination of a
COBOL statement. In the grep statement each line terminator like <LF>
will be changed to a space when not preceded by a dot.

In that way grepping on source and destination fields will be more
simple with a regular expression.

BTW, since some recent sed versions the --in-place option can be used to
avoid temporary files.

> 2. Career/Political Question -
> - What do I do now? I mentioned this tool capability to one low-level
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> on a machine, but I can't make "copies" of it onto a CD... :-)

I don't have read the license of your software as we don't have
MS-Windows software on our networks, but both grep and sed are
available in multiple Free Software servions, as in you have the right
to study, modify and redistribute it like you want.

But to give rest to others problems, you might select an older box,
install a Linux distribution, a BSD version or even an OpenSolaris
version on it and run FTP, grep and sed without retributions.

Good luck with your 5 figure savings. :-)

#### ↳ Re: grep'ing CoBOL source

- **From:** "Ira Baxter" <idbaxter@semdesigns.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-13T10:39:02-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<odqdnc2xyeCK-vjRnZ2dnUVZ_gSdnZ2d@giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<098a698a-a3da-4e55-b6b5-12a58ee646f9@d8g2000yqf.googlegroups.com>`

```
"Ken" <klshafer@att.net> wrote in message 
news:098a698a-a3da-4e55-b6b5-12a58ee646f9@d8g2000yqf.googlegroups.com...
>
> My shop (about 20-25 developers, and another 20-25 business analysts,
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> sed -n /ZERO/p <blkboxto.txt >blkboxzerosto.txt

[finding essentially MOVE ZERO/SPACE to XFER-BLK-... targets]

>It executed and scanned the 5000 COPYLIBs in less than two
>minutes :-).

Yes, grep was invented so people could fish around in big piles of code
quickly.    And that's really useful, as Ken has shown.

The good news is that grep is pretty fast.   The not so good
news is that is only pretty fast; he had to run it bunch of times.
Note he was piping the results to temp files to record what he did,
so he could decide if the answer was good.   He didn't say it,
but presumably he had to go open the files that appeared interesting
to verify his hits.

There are alternatives to grep which are faster and more convenient.

They are called "source code search engines".
OpenGrok (hub.opensolaris.org/bin/view/Project+opengrok)
is one of them (opensource).  It indexes and will thus search for 
identifiers very quickly;
it does this by knowing enough about the various langauges you
might work with to pick out identifiers.  It also
searches for abitrary strings with regexes at grep-like speeds,
and will show you lists of hits and offer to show you the code
directly.

Another is our Source Code Search Engine (commercial)
(www.semanticdesigns.com/Products/SearchEngine)
The SCSE knows the lexical structure (language tokens:
keywords, identifiers, numbers, comments, strings, whitespace)
of a variety of languages [e.g., Java, C#, C++, ) but
especially including COBOL
(and specifically AS400 and IBM Enterprise COBOL).
SCSE indexes *everything*, so it has lightning fast searches
for identifiers and combinations of tokens for complex queries.

Ken's search could have been written to the SCSE as:

    'MOVE'  ('ZERO'|'SPACES') 'TO' I=XFER-BLK-BOX-ACC*

(In fact for this query you don't really  need the 'MOVE' keyword).
You don't need to say anything about the whitespace, because
SCSE knows that whitespace isn't interesting (by definition,
in *any* language, for each langauge's defintiion of whitespace).
You don't need to worry about line breaks or formatting, because
they are whitespace, too.    Response times are seconds
across millions of lines of code, thousands of files.

Instead of temp files, he would have seen a GUI list of individual
lines across *all* files containing his hits; a single click from there
would pull up the the source file with the hit highlighted.

Such tools really are convenient ways to get "good information,
really fast".   I think the other tool Ken mentioned is one that
brings up "great information (maybe), much more slowly".

-- IDB
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: grep'ing CoBOL source

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-08-17T23:15:27+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8cv9ahFa0uU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<098a698a-a3da-4e55-b6b5-12a58ee646f9@d8g2000yqf.googlegroups.com> <f676deda-3d7e-4157-95ca-d74902256a2c@5g2000yqz.googlegroups.com>`

```
Derek Schrock wrote:
> On Aug 12, 9:13 pm, Ken <klsha...@att.net> wrote:
>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> grep -E "\bXFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT\b" file ...

Unfortunately, that won't catch the case where it is followed by a full stop 
or comma...

I'm not familiar with Grep, but I do have a nodding acquaintance with 
regular expressions. (At least, in a MS .Net environment...)

An expression can be told to look across multiple lines... ?m:
it can be told to ignore whitespace... ?x:
and it can be told to ignore match case... ?i:

So, a MATCH expression :   ?m:?x:?i:XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT

Applied to the following code snippet:

move spaces to
               XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT,
               dname-1,
               dname2
more code...
move something to XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT

...will match both instances of XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT. But it doesn't take 
into account the context; match is purely on target string.

if you want to find instances of space, spaces, zero, zeroes or zeros being 
moved to the target, the corresponding expression would be:

?m:?x:?i:(space|zero).*(\r\n|.*).*XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT

This will match on the first instance in the above, and both instances in:

move spaces to
               XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT,
               dname-1,
               dname2
more code...
move zeroes to XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT

BUT, it won't match the first instance in:

move spaces to
               dname,
               XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT,
               dname-1,
               dname2
more code...
move zeroes to XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT

To enhance it so it will find the target in a string of datanames,  where 
the target isn't necessarily the first one, retaining the context (Move 
spaces/zeros etc), and across multiple lines,  you would need:

?m:?x:?i:(?:space|zero.*\w*.*\r\n)*.*(XFER-BLK-BOX-ACCOUNT)

This finds both instances of the above and it doesn't matter where the 
target is in a list of datanames.However, it doesn't match references to the 
target that do NOT involve spaces or zeros.

I use an Australian tool called Rad Software Regular Expression designer 
downloadable for free from http://www.radsoftware.com.au/regexdesigner/ to 
test and play with Regexes.

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<098a698a-a3da-4e55-b6b5-12a58ee646f9@d8g2000yqf.googlegroups.com> <f676deda-3d7e-4157-95ca-d74902256a2c@5g2000yqz.googlegroups.com> <8cv9ahFa0uU1@mid.individual.net> <b8338991-24d1-44f8-a606-ce0e75f71fa4@5g2000yqz.googlegroups.com> <2bb21d76-2d19-4a12-9e49-a74036ccd909@x21g2000yqa.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Thu, 19 Aug 2010 05:10:47 -0700 (PDT), Alistair
<alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:

>According to my Unix book (O'Reilly) AWK, NAWK and GAWK use \n to be
>newline and \r to be carriage return. The implication being that
>newline is not an implicit carriage return.

If you write an editor to be used on a Mac, it needs to be able to
handle classic Mac & Unix endings and probably Windows as well.

DOS (and Windows)... uses a carriage return and line-feed character
pair (= 2 bytes with hexadecimal value 0D and 0A).
UNIX ... uses just the line-feed character (= 1 byte with hexadecimal
value 0A).
MAC (Apple) ... uses just the carriage return character (= 1 byte with
hexadecimal value 0D).

But no need to handle EBCDIC conventions.

The most problem makes the MAC line ending type when combined with DOS
line endings and the file contains now CR+CR+LF. How should an
application read that now? As 2 blank MAC lines and 1 blank UNIX line
(= 3 blank lines), as 1 blank MAC line and 1 blank DOS line (= 2 blank
lines) or as mistake in line endings and therefore display the first
CR as character and interpret the remaining CR+LF pair as DOS line
ending.
```

<a id="t-fc885943ee"></a>
## Thread: Is Cobol Dead? — 5 this month / 18 total

_Topics: [`COBOL's future, legacy, and obsolescence`](topics.md#future)_

### Is Cobol Dead?

- **From:** Casey <nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-17T03:05:31-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1909374165303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>`

```
Please forgive; I don't see a FAQ, and I haven't posted to the usenet in
11 years (roughly.) I may not be working the new-fangled mobile usenet
reader correctly, (is this thing on?) so if I'm violating any FAQ rules,
please have mercy. I'm delighted to see that it's popularity has waned
enough that most of the spam has at last gone away:)

I'm also completely new to COBOL. I spend my work day dealing mostly
with Ruby, Perl, Java, SQL, Javascript, and the markup crap (though I've
managed to steer my way away from most of the god forsaken web front end
stuff at last.) 

Check it out: COBOL just got a new user. Why? Because I've never had a
chance to learn it, it had meteoric popularity once, and there must have
been a reason for that. Also: I'm intrigued by the faux-English syntax.
In any event, in the network age, programmers from all corners are
discovering the legendary "lost languages" with unbridled enthusiasm.

After pulling down the list of messages, I couldn't help noticing the
funeral thread. 

Are we completely sure it's dead? Maybe all COBOL really needs is one
really radical, out of box idea. Something genuinely new that moves the
needle for the language. A better story on slashdot than "lots of money
maintaining COBOL code as there's almost no one left who knows it" or
"startup specializes in automatic translation of legacy COBOL systems to
Java."

Why not burn the disk packs and start over? 

Lisp got continuation based web frameworks, which make modeling control
flows through web applications much less painful. People are starting to
write web apps in Lisp: but only because Seaside touched an untapped
nerve, having copied the technique. Seaside was written in Squeak, but
might not have been, were it not for the fact that Squeak was written in
itself, which made it ultra-malleable, and attractive to people who like
to hack on languages. Forth is all the rage again! The hipsters are all
raving about Factor, what with it's portable Smalltalk style
GUI/development environment, but the reason it all really works is that
it lifted the best ideas from CLOS: it's object-forth, and object in the
radical sense of the word, not the Java sense of the word; meanwhile, ML
is reborn with new syntax and ideas from various other languages as
Haskell. 

I would bet:

COBOL is too compatible. Being used primarily in business, breaking
compat with older standards has mired potential new variants in
unnecessary complexity. Why not do something radical and invent a
completely new Cobol? Something that retains the essence of what keeps
everyone here reading and posting to this list, without the accumulated
cruft which presents unneeded cognitive overhead for new people who want
to learn the language?

COBOL is too big. Too many keywords. This makes it hard to learn. For
decades, companies have saved money by hiring kids straight out of
school, and the simplest languages have begun to win in industry. This
is why I'm paid to look at Ruby. Why not find the smallest usable subset
of COBOL? Or better yet, why not throw out the idea of keywords all
together, and make everything user defined, and written in COBOL, a la
Forth or Smalltalk?

Here's a whopper: what might COBOL be like without static typing? What
might COBOL look like with duck typing? These days, web companies want
to be able to make small, iterative product cycles go out the door
quickly, and the mission criticality is generally lower, as hot fixing
has become a way of life. These requirements regularly eclipse the
confidence that a business can derive by eliminating the category of
bugs that go away when one checks types at compile time. The performance
advantages of inlining functions make far less of a difference when the
hardware is so much cheaper than the time of the development team, and
when the dynamic optimization techniques developed for Self have in most
cases mitigated the worst overhead of dynamic systems. Besides, Ruby is
about as slow as a tortoise (though it doesn't need to be) and it's been
all the rage lately.

One last completely heretical idea: run it in the browser. V8 takes
advantage of the best compiler technologies of the last 20 years; if
your compiler is any good, the benches should blow away what you had at
the end of the eighties, which was good enough then. Implement COBOL in
Javascript. If you implement the language in Javascript, your programs
will live forever (well, until we're all dead anyway.)

If you made it all the way to the end, I hope you enjoyed my crazytalk.
Thanks for listening:)
```

#### ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead?

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-17T05:31:37-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<fytao.37589$Is6.2908@en-nntp-13.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<1909374165303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>`

```
"Casey" <nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:1909374165303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com...
<snip>
> Here's a whopper: what might COBOL be like without static typing? What
> might COBOL look like with duck typing? These days, web companies want
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>
<snip>

I doubt that COBOL will ever get rid of static typing, BUT it also has 
"dynamic" typing.  In the 2002 ANSI/ISO COBOL Standard, TYPEDEF and TYPE 
phrases were added.  There are also CALL Prototypes for "CALL conformance 
checking" at compile-time. These, along with Object Orientation and user 
defined functions add some of the "newer but not so new" programming 
language features that were missing in the 1985 COBOL Standard.

Several (not all) existing vendors and providers have some or all of these 
features.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead?

- **From:** e p chandler <epc8@juno.com>

On Aug 17, 8:05 am, Alistair <alist...@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
> On Aug 17, 9:05 am, Casey <nocannedmeat.casey.obrie...@gmail.com>
> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> character in one shell has a different meaning in another shell.

I'm not a regular COBOL programmer, but I *am* happy that I taught
myself parts of the language. I have a better understanding of:

- representations of data and data conversion issues
- databases
- business data processing

Let me cite two short examples.

1. An old Fortran program used keyed access on VMS to access a
database. Emulating some of this functionality was the only option.
(Standard Fortran has no such thing.) I tried to explain ISAM to the
other programmer, but he did not have the background. Understanding
COBOL helped me write the Fortran program I needed. [Knowing some
dBase3+ did not hurt, either.]

2. A customer gave me an Excel spreadsheet with a key field in col 1
and data in col 2. The assignment was to group data in col 2 for
identical key values in col 1. The data was pre-grouped by key. So I
was able to write fairly simple control-break logic in VBA thanks to
having seen it in COBOL.

IMO there is still a reason to TEACH COBOL.

----- e

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead?

- **From:** marktyers <marktyers@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-27T09:33:06-07:00

Anonymous <cripto@ecn.org> wrote:

> It's great for what it is for, but outside of it's envelope? You don't
> wanna go there.

What's the envelope? Why's don't I want to go outside it? Please
explain! I'm interested exactly in what the envelope is, and why it's
hard to get outside. 
 
>> In any event, in the network age, programmers from all corners are
>> discovering the legendary "lost languages" with unbridled enthusiasm.
> 
> Ada is one to look at then.

Ada was shoved down my throat by educators with funding from an air
force base when I was a kid. I hated it then. I wanted to write C++, and
I didn't see the value in it then. I kicked out and did Perl at a
startup instead.

I found the value in it on the job many years later when I saw a block
of C# code that basically caught all exceptions and handled them by
doing nothing, leaving the application in an undefined state.

Ada still influenced my thinking such that I was confused by the fact
that the compiler would even compile that idiom. I was blown away.

Ada introduced a certain brain damage in my early life: the assumption
that the compiler would wipe people's asses, which isn't terribly true
outside of Ada; most early bound languages will wipe one's ass with
regard to type checking, but that's mostly all you can get out of it.

> COBOL

there anywhere I can go looking for that?

magnitude more than people outside of COBOL are used to. I hit this
point where I didn't want any reserved words anymore, but that's mostly
that I've slammed into the wall with programming idioms; I'm sick of
there being a difference between the code that I write and the
"hardcode." 

I'm sick of immutable black boxes. I think they inhibit progress. But,
then again, I'm a fringe-nutbag:)

```
Casey wrote:
> Anonymous <cripto@ecn.org> wrote:
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> hard to get outside.

Perhaps Anonymous is referring to the "new envelope" which COBOL was never 
designed for and which didn't exist when it WAS designed. Although modern 
COBOL has been enhanced to participate in this arena 
(http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21 ), it has been left behind by other 
languages which do it better. The choices for most corporates seem to be 
replacement with a package, replacement with a language more suited to 
networking, or outsourcing of IT requirements. Neverthless, it is possible 
to "salvage" a large amount of existing COBOL code by using a modern OO 
COBOL compiler to create components and objects that encapsulate working 
functionality. Sometimes this can prepresent a significant saving over 
complete redevelopment in a new language. Even if it doesn't, it is nice to 
have the choice...  Obviously, this is speculation on my part and Anonymous 
would need to confirm exactly what he meant.
>
>>> In any event, in the network age, programmers from all corners are
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>
There are occasions when you might want to do that. The application is not 
in an undefined state; in fact the state is clearly defined by the type of 
exception that was caught, assuming the programmer bothered to analyse it. 
For example, a non fatal exception that doesn't prevent the required 
processing from continuing, may not be something you want to raise to a user 
and stop processing for. In the cases where it isn't legitimate do you blame 
the language or blame the programmer? I write C# all the time and I enjoy 
doing so very much. I'd rather have a language that let's me do what I want 
to do than have a language that acts like Nanny and "knows best"...

I avoid writing early bound code like the plague in ANY language, largely 
because I find the compliance checking more of a pain than a help... I'll 
trade efficiency for flexibility any time, with respect to commercial 
applications.

>
> Ada still influenced my thinking such that I was confused by the fact
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> there anywhere I can go looking for that?

There really aren't THAT many verbs in COBOL. It's a bit like SQL; many 
people don't realise that SQL only has a handful of actual commands, because 
there are many possible variations on the commands available. (I have some 
software that needed to check for COBOL reserved words when parsing text. I 
was amazed to find the list included around 630 words. If you look at that 
you think it must be a very complex language. But the actual constructs used 
are not so and even untrained programmers can learn to write COBOL at least 
as quickly as someone can learn Basic. (Note, I said "write" not 
"program"...)

The answer to your question is to look through existing samples of COBOL 
code. You will find that the most used verbs are MOVE and PERFORM. A 4000 
line program will probably have thousands of them...

>
> Please forgive the "too many keywords" statement. It's just an order
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> "hardcode."

Have you considered using Assembler?
>
> I'm sick of immutable black boxes. I think they inhibit progress. But,
> then again, I'm a fringe-nutbag:)

I don't think that makes you a nutbag, but it may indicate a degree of 
myopia :-)  My experience has been that "black boxes" (components) are an 
excellent way of building and implementing computer systems. In much the 
same way that the electronics industry found large savings in implementing 
standard reusable components, the same is true for software.  For a 
component to be useful it SHOULD be immutable, with consistent behaviours 
(methods) and a consistent interface. Internal properties of the component 
can be used to modify its behaviours and provide more flexibility, and new 
behaviours can be added where a small deviation from an existing behaviour 
is required (extension of methods). It is much better than modifying 
integrated code where you are likely to kick off side effects that may not 
be immediately obvious...
>
>> I enjoyed reading your post. I don't agree with most of your (or
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> intelligent life in the universe!

I never cease to wonder at how, when you agree with someone, they consider 
you intelligent... :-)

Nevertheless, I didn't violently disagree with what you said. Does that make 
me just "slow"... :-)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead? <190937416 5303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2010-08-19T13:02:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i4ja0l$qe0$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<8d235rF5f1U1@mid.individual.net> <751033128303876552.511564nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>`

```
In article <751033128303876552.511564nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>,
Casey  <nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r@gmail.com> wrote:
>Comments below...

[snip]

>In this case, it was catching a generic exception and handling it
>without doing anything. If it'd at least logged the exception, I would
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>curious misconception that I had once as a result of growing up on Ada.

What you're calling a 'misconception' here seems to be what I was taught, 
e'er-so-long ago, to call 'proper error-checking'.  Given that Data Are 
Sacred and processing them involves ascertaining their ascribing to the 
Current Rules of Sanctity - a set of rules that changes as per management 
fiat, user request, legal requirement or any one of a host of Really Good 
Reasons - the One True Way of insuring data's Sanctity is by rigorous (and 
often negative) testing.

Consider the classic example of sex.

(A brief pause will be permitted for those who feel the need to giggle 
when sex is mentioned.  This is the *only* such pause as will be allowed 
for this behavior and the rest of the example will be written at normal 
pace.)

Away back when there were two possibilities for this: M(ale) or F(emale).  
If one adheres to the dictum that 'The Source of Data is Always 
Unreliable' then it would not be uncommon to see code along similar to:

(the following example is written in the style of Away Back When)

01  WK-EMP-REC.
...
    05  WK-EMPREC-SEX  PIC X(01).
        88  VALID-SEX  VALUES 'M' 'F'.
        88  MALE-SEX   VALUE 'M'.
        88  FEMALE-SEX VALUE 'F'.
...
    READ A10752-EMPLOYEE-MASTER-EXTRACT
     INTO WK-EMP-REC
      AT END MOVE 'Y' TO NO-MORE-INPUT
       GO TO 5000-MAIN-PROCESS-EX.
...(various edit routines)
    IF NOT VALID-SEX
        PERFORM 8172-SEX-ERROR THRU 8172-EX
        GO TO   5000-MAIN-PROCESS-EX.
    IF FEMALE-SEX
        PERFORM 5348-PROCESS-FEMALE-EMPL THRU 5348-EX
    ELSE
        PERFORM 5297-PROCESS-MALE-EMPL   THRU 5297-EX.
...(et and cetera)

Notice the first thing tested is the NOT VALID-SEX condition.  The reason 
for this is that CPU time Costs Money and if there's a reason not to burn 
cycles checking for nonexistent conditions; if an employee has a sex-code 
of 'R' then the condition gets flagged and one moves on to the next.

Some shops had rules against negative condition checking; coding around 
that would be achieved by using NEXT SENTENCE... which got kind of broken 
in 1985 but that's only a couple of decades and change ago.

[snip]

>I suppose to clarify what I really want is to be able to monkey patch
>irresponsibly at run time, mainly when testing.

It sounds like you are looking for your code to implement the DWIM (Do 
What I Mean) command and that's not part of any Standard with which I'm 
familiar.  Making code do non-Standard stuff has a curious place in 
Business (CO*B*OL) environments and might be discussed in another 
thread... but the language wasn't really designed to do such things.

DD
```

In article <rr1r66t7npdhn59vnok3v7m861rej6qioh@4ax.com>,
Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
>On Thu, 19 Aug 2010 13:02:13 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>Teehee.

Good that you made use of it, Mr Brazee... this is actually because of... 
A Story!

During my Kollidj Daze some of the works of Lavoisier were required 
reading and the most common edition of this used an Olde Stylee Englishe 
font, one in which the 'long s' (resembling the lower-case 'f') was used.  
The tutor... errrrr, professor began the class with the instruction of 
'Those of you using the (name) edition will now turn to Page (m), 
paragraph (n), sentence (o) and read to yourself how Lavoisier describes 
how one 'sucks the air out the tube'.

'You now have five minutes for laughter, after which none will be 
permitted on this particular matter.'

>
>>Away back when there were two possibilities for this: M(ale) or F(emale).  
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
>necessary.

That's an argument for 'tuned' code (and one which has encountered some 
resistance along the lines of 'do not expend programming effort for 
minimal gains').  Given that in different applications the population can 
be mixed, preponderantly male or preponderantly female a 'Generic 
Selection' (and, of course, Ladies First) was used for this demonstration.

>
>I'd like to see the compiled code for these checks.

I believe that on both IKFCBL00 and IGYCRCYTL all compares involving a 
single-character field generate a CLI and a BC.  Since VALID-SEX involves 
two comparisons ('M' and 'F') there'd be two CLIs and two BCs.

DD

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead? <190937416 5303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-08-22T01:24:09+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8da2brFbn6U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<8d235rF5f1U1@mid.individual.net> <rr1r66t7npdhn59vnok3v7m861rej6qioh@4ax.com> <i4lroe$qls$1@reader1.panix.com> <o43t661fev2248gsssp5d02t7gccgb91ov@4ax.com> <i4m4u6$pja$1@reader1.panix.com> <6vrt66pqpmj0t664t6o6aqqpj9vfrph0fk@4ax.com> <8d8q7eFo0lU1@mid.individual.net> <o5Mbo.66027$pX3.59827@en-nntp-11.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Bill Klein wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:8d8q7eFo0lU1@mid.individual.net...
…[52 more quoted lines elided]…
> differently?

I would not have PERFORM... and ELSE  (or ANY other action)  before the next 
IF.

The form of my nesting is as a decision tree, with only IF nested, only in 
the TRUE branches:

if < not condition 1>
    if <not condition  2>
        if  <not condition  3>
             action 4
        else
              action 3
... etcetera.

I learned this form from Dr. Fritz D. McCameron of the University of 
Louisiana in 1969. It has served me well in the ensuing decades :-) It is 
sad that people ban constructs like this instead of teaching programmers how 
to use them properly.

Pete.
```

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-21T09:02:55-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i0Rbo.67141$pX3.64601@en-nntp-11.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<8d235rF5f1U1@mid.individual.net> <rr1r66t7npdhn59vnok3v7m861rej6qioh@4ax.com> <i4lroe$qls$1@reader1.panix.com> <o43t661fev2248gsssp5d02t7gccgb91ov@4ax.com> <i4m4u6$pja$1@reader1.panix.com> <6vrt66pqpmj0t664t6o6aqqpj9vfrph0fk@4ax.com> <8d8q7eFo0lU1@mid.individual.net> <o5Mbo.66027$pX3.59827@en-nntp-11.dc1.easynews.com> <8da2brFbn6U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:8da2brFbn6U1@mid.individual.net...
> Bill Klein wrote:
>> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
…[74 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.
Once upon a time (and long ago), I was "taught" then when nesting "IF's" you 
should try to put the "most likely" TRUE test 9and its correspond action) 
first and then go down the "probablity" chain for subsequent IF's, e.g.

If Highly-likely
    Perform  Lots-of-times
Else
   If Sometimes
      Perform   On-Occassion
  Else
    If Rarely
          Perform  Not-Very-Often
    Else
         Perform Should-never-Happen-Error-Routine
    End-IF
  End-IF
End-IF

I don't know how many (milli-)seconds this ever saved in multi-million 
record batch programs, but it was the way that I was taught.  The other 
"approach" to nested IF's that I learned (at one time) was for character 
based screen processing, do error checking from the bottom of the screen 
fields upwards.   I never really did get in the habit of this one - even 
when doing CICS and IMS "green screen" applications.  Rather, I TENDED to 
check for "most common errors" first.
```

```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:cfef3c81-da71-4a72-896a-6af18d97e429@l20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com...
On Aug 21, 3:02 pm, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:

> I don't know how many (milli-)seconds this ever saved in multi-million
> record batch programs, but it was the way that I was taught. The other
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> check for "most common errors" first.

What was the rationale behind error checking from the bottom of the
screen upwards?

In CICS when you find an error in a field on a screen you move a minus one 
to the length attribute for the field.  This affects the placement of the 
cursor when the screeen is displayed.  Editing the fields from the bottom of 
the screen to the top of the screen, right to left, results in placing the 
cursor in the top most field that is in error.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Nested IF's (was: Is Cobol Dead?

_(thread depth: 17)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2010-08-23T16:07:46+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i4u6ci$2am$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<i4lroe$qls$1@reader1.panix.com> <cfef3c81-da71-4a72-896a-6af18d97e429@l20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <4sCdnfXqaJq_bvLRnZ2dnUVZ_o2dnZ2d@earthlink.com> <nh05769rneh6dltdikctpt3ul538kk286n@4ax.com>`

```
In article <nh05769rneh6dltdikctpt3ul538kk286n@4ax.com>,
Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
>On Sat, 21 Aug 2010 11:39:19 -0400, "Charles Hottel"
><chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>this doesn't work.

The 'when' of 'until you find an error' is, if an error is found, 
satisfied no matter where one begins in a list.

Regarding how one looks for and indicates errors in CICS:  I recall being 
taught that how one does this can be as simple as 'how do all the other 
programs do it?' to a delicate art which deals with input mode (is the 
entry-clerk working from stacks of paper for entry, stacks of paper for 
corrections, a pair of headsets and a voice, etc), error analysis (deadly, 
'are you sure', review process), corporate culture and so on.  Searching 
archives shows I hinted at this a few years back in 
<http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/ee3155845f97f014?dmode=source> 
when I posted:

--begin quoted text:

(Next week's topic of discussion for The Ergonomics of Screen Design is 
'Flagging Errors: Inverse Video, Flashing - Emphatic Indicator or Sadist's 
Tool?') 

--end quoted text

... and it seems that next week was a goodly bit in coming.

It was a goodly while go, in the days of Away Back When... and I was 
tasked with coding a new data-entry screen for a new form.  This was the 
pet project of a Corner-Office Idiot so I had to endure some face-to-face 
time with him and one of the things I asked was 'How do you want errors to 
be indicated?'

His response of a waved hand and 'Make 'em obvious' was something I'd even 
then learned to translate into 'No matter what you do it'll be wrong and 
then I'll get to look important ordering you to do Something Else and 
moaning in Managers' Meetings about how these moneygrubbing consultants 
can't get anything right just so they can bill more hours'...

... and so I said nothing else.  A week later he and I were standing 
behind the data-entry girl (they still called them 'girls', Away Back 
When') and after a few bits of back-and-forth ('Why didn't you put *this* 
over *there* and *that* over *here*?'  'If you look at the form *this* is 
over *here* and *that* is over *there*; the screen is designed to mimic 
the form so that the clerk's eyes flow in the same, most efficient 
fashion.') the lass keyed in the data and whacked <Enter>...

... resulting in every error coming back in flashing inverse video, CAPS 
BLARING... she gave a bit of a jump and started weeping and the COI 
blurbled 'what is all *that* crap?'

'That's what you asked for, Mr Jones; when I asked how to deal with errors 
you told me to 'make 'em obvious'.'

'Well... not *that* obvious, do something else.'

Having demonstrated that he could command others to do better work he left 
me to my own devices and I fell back on the standard 'check from the 
bottom up; if there's an error move -1 to the length, set the attribute 
of the field to bright and the attribute of the previous field to normal'.

DD
```

```
On Aug 24, 2:14 am, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Sat, 21 Aug 2010 11:39:19 -0400, "Charles Hottel"
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> this doesn't work.

Stopping the checks when you find the first error can lead to an
interaction for each field. OTOH the validity of later fields may
depend on the content of earlier ones.

I never used CICS, nor anything IBM mainframe, but the technique of
flagging errors from the bottom up was used with other block mode
screens, including COBOL Screen Section.

For my console based Cobol systems for the last 30 years, though, I
have almost exclusively used field-by-field processing as being the
most suitable.

As for the issue of whether it should be IF NOT 'M' or 88 levels or an
EVALUATE, I would use none of those. 30 years ago I decided that small
data items, such as Male/Female, Branch codes, Tax rates, etc should
all be dumped into a 'Decode File' using the field name as the major
key with the code value as the minor key. The client can then decide
which codes were valid and what description was shown by adding or
deleting records in that file using a single maintenance program.

It also allowed the valid values to be stepped through using a
function key in the field or listed to be chosen from. A common piece
of code does the validation for the field. Given the field name, if
the record exists then the field value is valid.

The tricky part is that the decode file has several fields in its
records and the maintenance program uses a control file to specify,
for each decode 'field name', the headings, and other details, for the
input along with help text to guide the usage. Thus one program can
maintain all these small data items.

Then I have a single major file maintenance program which will
maintain most large data files (customer, supplier, product, stock,
etc) and dynamically builds its screen layout based on control file
information specifying the field names, field heading, location in the
record, type, validation requirement, etc. For 'small data' items
validated using the decode file it is only necessary to specify
whether an existing record is required or optional, the description is
shown or is 'NOT FOUND' and the items can be stepped through.

Over the decades I have found this enables me to put together a system
in very short time. Some systems (e.g. order entry, invoicing,
debtors, etc) went from request to running live in 6 weeks.





> --
> "In no part of the constitution is more wisdom to be found,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> - James Madison

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead? <190937416 5303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-08-21T13:52:38+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8d8pr7Fma9U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<8d235rF5f1U1@mid.individual.net> <rr1r66t7npdhn59vnok3v7m861rej6qioh@4ax.com> <i4lroe$qls$1@reader1.panix.com> <o43t661fev2248gsssp5d02t7gccgb91ov@4ax.com> <i4m4u6$pja$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <o43t661fev2248gsssp5d02t7gccgb91ov@4ax.com>,
> Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>>> CLIs and two BCs.

Yep, but there is no need for the VALID-SEX check at all.

If you care about this sort of thing (and I generally don't because other 
languages handle it differently),
a simple decision tree, implemented via a correctly nested COBOL IF, removes 
the need for the VALID-SEX test. (In other circumstances it would remove the 
need for ANY "blanket" validation test; such tests are never required iof 
the logic handles all cases.)

IF NOT MALE
     IF NOT FEMALE
          PERFORM ERROR-PROCESS
     ELSE
            PERFORM FEMALE-PROCESS
     ENDIF
ELSE
     PERFORM MALE-PROCESS
END-IF

Sadly, many COBOL shops, instead of teaching people how to use things like 
negated conditions and nested IFs, simply ban them.(I call this "writing 
COBOL for the LCD"...) The above code is simple, easily maintained (you can 
add new options to it by further nesting), and can be optimized to test 
things in the order they are most likely to occur.

Consider the case where the program processes data from a population where 
people consider their sexuality to be: 70% straight female, 20%  straight 
male, and 10% gay...:

The COBOL code above is not only easily amended but it can be easily 
optimized too...

IF NOT FEMALE
     IF NOT MALE
          IF NOT GAY
              PERFORM ERROR-PROCESS
          ELSE
              PERFORM GAY-PROCESS
          ENDIF
     ELSE
          PERFORM MALE-PROCESS
     ENDIF
ELSE
     PERFORM FEMALE-PROCESS
END-IF

It will still generate CLC and BC. If you normally worry about that sort of 
thing, remove the 88 levels and force CLI and BC...

IF SEX NOT =  'F'
     IF SEX NOT = 'M'
          IF NOT GAY
              PERFORM ERROR-PROCESS
          ELSE
              PERFORM GAY-PROCESS
          ENDIF
     ELSE
          PERFORM MALE-PROCESS
     ENDIF
ELSE
     PERFORM FEMALE-PROCESS
END-IF

As far as I know, that's about the best you will do in COBOL on an IBM 
mainframe, but I'll happily stand corrected if someone cares to show an 
improvement over the above.

No 88 levels, no unnecessary branches or ELSEs, minimal source code (the 
less there is, the easier it should be to assimilate and understand it, 
never mind compile time efficiency)... and so on.

And the bottom line is that writing code is NOT a straightforward process 
UNLESS you have total responsibility for it, and control over it, at which 
point you can apply whatever your personal priorities are). There are SO 
many factors involved and different people weight them differently.

Is code wrong? Only if it doesn't work correctly.

Is one approach better than another?  Depends on what your criteria are.

Does it make sense to HATE one form and LOVE another? No. (The emotional 
reaction will depend largely on what you are accustomed to and familiar 
with.)

Should you refuse to code in accordance with stupid and restrictive 
installation standards, which were formulated by some LCD lead programmer? 
(I have been guilty of this...) No, you shouldn't do that. As Doc frequently 
points out, they are writing your paycheck. Do what they require. There is 
nothng to stop you pointing out a better way, but it is their right to pick 
it up or not. If it REALLY pisses you off, look for another job or go 
contract...

Not that it matters, but on two occasions where I smply ignored standards 
and coded what was better, they picked it up in code review and asked me to 
explain it. I did, and everyone agreed it was better. I was lucky, and it 
all depends on the culture of the organsation. If they believe control is 
more important than a better result (WHO's right over WHAT's right) then you 
are likely to be in trouble. But you probably won't be staying there very 
long anyway because good people don't really stay in bad organisations for 
long, and whether it is about coding or about something else, you are likely 
to become frustrated and not get the job satisfaction that you would if you 
were working with brighter, more positive people.

>>
>> That's what I would guess.   Assuming we really care about that level
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> the VALID-SEX check first.

Or removing it altogether. See above.
>
> (note, class, how different assumptions lead to opposite, equally
> defensible conclusions)

Yes, diversity is just as important in programming as it is in ethnicity... 
:-)

Pete.
```

> for a response.

You didn't read what I wrote. I said if "they considered their sexuality to 
be" in other words,"stated sexuality"  (for the purpose of this exercise) 
For the purpose of processing by this program, the theoretical population is 
what they SAY they are. They could, in fact be anything. And yes, 
bisexuality was not catered for because it was not a stated option. I'm 
hopeful that after seeing how the tree was easily extended to accommodate 
"gay" you would have no trouble in extending it further to accommodate 
"bisexual"... :-)
>
>> It will still generate CLC and BC. If you normally worry about that
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…

Because it takes more source code, and it doesn't give me a chance to 
demonstrate a properly nested IF... :-). (nevertheless, I do use 
EVALUATE...)

EVALUATE SEX
     WHEN FEMALE
              PERFORM FEMALE PROCESS
     WHEN MALE
              PERFORM MALE PROCESS
      WHEN GAY
              PERFORM GAY-PROCESS
       WHEN  OTHER
              PERFORM ERROR-PROCESS
END EVALUATE

It only takes one line more of source, but it doesn't look as cool and I 
like decision trees to look like trees. You lost this by reformatting the 
original construct when quoting.

Why not use GO TO... DEPENDING ON ?  (There was a time when we did...) :-)

Pete.

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead? <190937416 5303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2010-08-21T14:30:51+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i4onur$hgm$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<8d235rF5f1U1@mid.individual.net> <o43t661fev2248gsssp5d02t7gccgb91ov@4ax.com> <i4m4u6$pja$1@reader1.panix.com> <8d8pr7Fma9U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <8d8pr7Fma9U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>> In article <o43t661fev2248gsssp5d02t7gccgb91ov@4ax.com>,
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
>END-IF

It seems that more than one person isn't familiar with - or, perhaps, has 
a memory weakened by (whatever activities not mentioned in polite society 
which are purported to weaken memory) - and ignored/forgotten the 
specific, albeit parenthetic notation given in 
<http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/38abd7f3c3011faf?dmode=source) 
of:

--begin quoted text:

(the following example is written in the style of Away Back When)

--end quoted text

... and cheerily trot out the equivalent of 'Well, Genetic Manipulation 
would have solved this difficulty of 1972 with the simple technique of...'

Away Back When the compiler would have rejected anything but upper case.
  
Away Back When the compiler would have rejected scope-delimiters.

Away Back When the compiler would have rejected an EVALUATE.

The code was written, explicitly, in the style of Away Back When.

[snip]

>If you normally worry about that sort of 
>thing, remove the 88 levels and force CLI and BC...
>
>IF SEX NOT =  'F'
>     IF SEX NOT = 'M'

Hard-coded PROCEDURE DIVISION literal, Mr Dashwood?  I've run across more 
sites that forbid those than negative condition-checks.

[snip]

>No 88 levels, no unnecessary branches or ELSEs, minimal source code (the 
>less there is, the easier it should be to assimilate and understand it, 
>never mind compile time efficiency)... and so on.

Not much use as an example to expand technique, either, unless one's 
student assimilates atomised experience.  I know it is a Matter of Style 
but I'll wade forward into this Big Muddy.

Granted, the 88 has opponents and detractors.  Permit me to continue my 
example so that the detractors might have more to shake their heads over, 
point and laugh at or say 'No, all ya gotta do is reach *over* your head 
with your *left* hand and you can scratch your right ear easily, no need 
to (*gasp*) put down your beer!'.

The concept of condition check being demonstrated by the previous, trivial 
example of sex - remember, time for giggling has expired - the next 
example which might be of value to consider is that of location.  In the 
United States of American there have been - despite the mis-speaking of 
politicians - 50 states since 1959.  In 1963 two-letter abbreviations for 
these were issued by the United States Post Office Department:

AL = Alabama
AK = Alaska
AZ = Arizona
...
WY = Wyoming

(for the purpose of demonstration entities such as Guam, Palau and the US 
Minor Outlying Islands are ignored)

Since many programs need to validate states the WORKING-STORAGE for this 
list is kept in a COPY member... my, isn't that an easy way to introduce 
*that* concept, as well?  But moving onwards...

... consider how this example builds on VALID-SEX, extending it to 
VALID-STATE.  In this case one checks against not two but fifty 
possibilities with a single statement.

The lesson then expands to keeping the various states not as a single 88 
but as a series of 88s in a table, to be PERFORMed THRU... and then 
SEARCHed... and then SEARCH ALLed...

... and then a number is associated with each STATE, so that 
VALID-STATE(SUB1) yields the Federal Information Processing Standard 
(FIPS) code and the concept of all data at a given level of a table is 
associated with the same subscript displacement from its base...

... and then one expands to dynamic tables, for, say, description of parts 
and their numbers... and the numbers are nicely packed down in order to 
save space in the LINKAGE SECTION when they get passed from program to 
program... and from there one might readily proceed to efficient use of 
the DFHCOMMAREA...

... or, on the other hand, one can ignore 88s, hardcode literals and 
scan/change each and every program (oh... and remember that old Luigi used 
-CDE, not -CODE) when something new is required.

There are arguments for, and against styles... both in programs and in 
UseNet postings.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is Cobol Dead? <190937416 5303721544.634709nocannedmeat.casey.obrien.r-gmail.com@news.giganews.com>

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-18T07:04:09-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3%Pao.20459$Yn5.1959@en-nntp-14.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<20100817160303.D1C3C1A7AE3@www.ecn.org> <589440f0-85de-4788-83e9-6e7a8d35bd10@l20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <i4gf1q$54h$2@reader1.panix.com>`

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:i4gf1q$54h$2@reader1.panix.com...
> In article 
> <589440f0-85de-4788-83e9-6e7a8d35bd10@l20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>,
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> that it here addresses.
<snip>

Interesting "semantic" question:

When talking about "programming languages in general *AND* COBOL"
  does "reserved word" mean "Key words"?

There are reserved words in COBOl which are NOT (in COBOL usage) "Key 
Words", i.e. they are never signficant. For example, "is" is ALWAYS an 
optional word in syntax (which doesn't mean that it can be placed wherever 
you want it).  There are also "English language equivalents" that add to the 
number of reserved words, e.g. "zero" and "zeros" and "zeroes"; not to 
mention "is" and "are".

Also, since 1989, there have been new words "reserved in context".  In 1989, 
the word "function" was introduced as such a word; in the 2002 Standard, 
there are lots more like this.

None of these changes the fact that there ARE more than 50 "key words" much 
less "reserved words".
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8d380uF2rvU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<20100817160303.D1C3C1A7AE3@www.ecn.org> <589440f0-85de-4788-83e9-6e7a8d35bd10@l20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <i4gf1q$54h$2@reader1.panix.com> <3%Pao.20459$Yn5.1959@en-nntp-14.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Bill Klein wrote:
> <docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message
> news:i4gf1q$54h$2@reader1.panix.com...
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> words" much less "reserved words".

I have a parser I wrote for COBOL which looks up a "reserved word" list with 
over 600 entries.

Your point that only some of these are "key words" is well taken.

I think it is important to remember that some of the "key words" are NOT in 
general usage by most COBOL sites. Communications module, Report Writer, and 
facilities that are no longer used but remain in the language (overlaying, 
ALTER, OTHERWISE...) all have words associated with them which are not 
really part of the "normal working set" that Casey was looking for.

I believe the best way to determine what this working set is, would be 
empirically by analysing and counting unique reserved word use in a large 
sample of actual working COBOL code. I don't believe there would be 600 such 
words; pidgin English only has 800 :-)

Pete.
```

<a id="t-a279af765a"></a>
## Thread: Arithmetic Sign In COBOL — 32 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Arithmetic Sign In COBOL

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-19T10:03:18-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8gdq669aqm908iaqpt57hpb0t7jhm5g50v@4ax.com>`

```

I thought this issue had gone away with the passing of the IBM-370
series of computers and COBOL-F!  However, I recently came across this
when I wrote a file that was being processed by another system and
that other system choked on one of my fields.  Here's the scenario:

In my program I have a file and one of the fields in the file is
defined as PIC S9(9)V99.  We'll call it TRAN-AMT.

Throughout the program,  calculations are done and eventually a final
result is moved to TRAN-AMT.  The intermediary calculation fields are
all defined as PIC S9(11)V99  COMP-3.

The program that processes my file after I've written it is happy with
all of my fields.  The other system, not so much.  When it comes to
TRAN-AMT it says it isn't numeric and moves zeroes to it making it
lifeless as well as useless.  I do not have access to the other
system's source, so I don't know what definitions it is using for my
fields.  My guess is they are PIC X something and they're doing an IF
NUMERIC type test on it.

Some research showed that the last byte of TRAN-AMT carried the
arithmetic sign of C plus whatever number.  For example if the last
byte was equal to 1, then in hex you would see C1.  This is a
perfectly valid numeric field, in most cases.  Sure if you browse the
file and see that field, that byte would display as an A instead of 1,
but nonetheless, it is still a valid numeric (as would D1 be).

I made some changes in field definitions within my program and the end
result was that TRAN-AMT would always carry the F sign in the last
byte.  It is not possible for the field to ever be negative.

My query to the group is, if you were in the same situation I found
myself in, what coding solution would you come up with?  I'm curious
as to how others may have solved this.

This is IBM mainframe, IBM Enterprise COBOL for Z/OS 4.2.0.

Regards,
```

#### ↳ Re: Arithmetic Sign In COBOL

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2010-08-19T14:44:59+00:00

```
In article <8gdq669aqm908iaqpt57hpb0t7jhm5g50v@4ax.com>,
SkippyPB  <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
>
>I thought this issue had gone away with the passing of the IBM-370
>series of computers and COBOL-F!

Wait a moment... 370s and COBOL-F have 'passed'?  Why, it seems like only 
yesterday that... ummmm... yesterday, that... well, yesterday was a day 
ago, ain't them kids knowed that?

>However, I recently came across this
>when I wrote a file that was being processed by another system and
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>fields.

Stop right there.  Using the Janitor's Analogy: if you don't have access 
to the room you cannot clean the room; if you do not have access to the 
other system's source you cannot debug the program.

Period.

Make this very, very clear to each and every Corner-Office Idiot who 
claims that you should be able to evaluate, diagnose and change code that 
you are not allowed to see.  

If this process has been running happily for a while and now, all of a 
sudden, the receiving system starts to puke on your input then it is 
reasonable to conclude that since your input has not changed the receiving 
system *has* changed.  *They* change it, *they* fix it or *they* give you 
access so you can fix it.

>My guess is they are PIC X something and they're doing an IF
>NUMERIC type test on it.

My guess is that you'll be lucky and find that a two-year programmer was 
browbeaten into an 'all ya gotta do' solution which blew up in his face.

>
>Some research showed that the last byte of TRAN-AMT carried the
>arithmetic sign of C plus whatever number.  For example if the last
>byte was equal to 1, then in hex you would see C1.  This is a
>perfectly valid numeric field, in most cases.

Eh?  Valid numeric fields are EBCDIC F0 through F9, anything beyond that 
is, I believe, implementor-specific.  Yes, IGYCRCTL will recognise X'123F' 
as a valid numeric IF it is defined as a COMP-3 field... but try filling 
that same field with X'12C1' and see what kind results you get.

Likewise, a field defined as S9 will hold X'C1' easily... move it to a pic 
X (as you mentioned) and (as the Germans say) 'Alles geht Kerflooie'.

>Sure if you browse the
>file and see that field, that byte would display as an A instead of 1,
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>as to how others may have solved this.

When sending to another system define all numeric outputs as something 
like PIC S9(9)V99 SIGN LEADING/TRAILING SEPARATE CHARACTER.  When (not 
'if') someone gets the bright idea to start ftp'ing these for 
spreadsheet-loading there should be nary a peep.

When it comes to debugging code that I do not have access to I tell folks 
to either get me access or find someone else... if I can fix stuff I am 
not allowed to 'lay the hands upon' then I'd make a better living than I 
do slinging code.

DD
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
> I am simply staggered that anyone is expected to fix code they have no
> access to.
>

Oh, it's not much different than fixing data...

I once had a file with multiple records for each test on an oil well. I had 
to write a program to summarize the tests.

Included in each record was the name of the well; I took the names for each 
report line from the name found in the first record.

The summary was correct but some of the names were garbled.

Investigation of the raw data revealed that after the first (or 
one-hundredth) test, somebody noticed the error in the well name and 
corrected it for subsequent records.

Mumble-mumble. Okay, modify the program to get the annotation from the LAST 
record in a well's series.

Bingo! All the annotations that were formerly garbage are now correct! But 
wait... Some of the annotations that were previously okay were now trashed 
(??).

Further investigation revealed that some of the well names started out okay, 
but due to streaming neutrinos from the sun or bit-rot, they decomposed into 
garbage.

So I counted the number of alphabetic characters in each record's well name. 
The record with the highest number was the one whose name was used on the 
printed annotation.
```

Quantum fluctuations do not generally manifest in the world we observe, 
although they obviously could if enough of them happened in the right place 
at the right moment. A small but finite chance. You might consider winning 
First Division Lotto 5 weekends in a row...

The answer to your question is: "Something got changed by the first attempt 
to run it."

> A system I worked on was described as having a black hole in to which
> library catalogue records fell (and were subsequently retained, or so
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> the black hole theory.

There is a fine line between belief and actuality. There are places in 
Africa where people get healed by the effect of crystals (once they believe 
the crystals will heal them.)

I like the story of the elderly man who married a Pretty Little Thing and 
was delighted when she announced she was pregnant. He regaled the members of 
his Club with the impending pregnancy and made sure everyone understood how 
potent he was.

Finally another old codger told a story...

" A few days ago I was walking in the woods on my estate when I saw a 
rabbit.  Just on a whimsical impulse, I pointed my walking stick at it and 
said: 'Bang!'  To my amazement, the rabbit fell down dead.

The following day, I was again in the woods, saw another rabbit, did the 
same again, and again, the rabbit dropped dead.

Yesterday, I was in the woods again looking for a rabbit. Finally, I saw 
one, pointed my walking stick at it, said:'Bang!' and it keeled over just 
like the others. Then I turned around and realised there was a man behind me 
with a real gun."

The bottom line is that sunspots and neutrinos don't affect computer systems 
any more than rabbits fall dead when you point sticks at them. (Neutrinos by 
their very nature don't interact with normal matter, passing straight 
through it, and sunspots could only affect a computer system by disrupting 
its wireless communications, not by changing its computations. Besides, 
there was no sunspot activity at the time, I checked...:-))

Pete.

some stinkwad, glad-handing do-nothing who calls *their* stinkwad, 
glad-handing do-nothing and mangles your scintillatingly clear technical 
requests into sorta-kinda-murfle-burbles that get passed on to a coder?

If you have to go through a do-nothing then do all you can - I would not 
suggest line-tapping (induction coils can be found with a bit of looking) 
or anything of that sort, no! - to find out the number of who is next on 
the list.  Get in contact with the next and do your best to find a coder.  
Have the coder send you the record layout they are expecting to receive OR 
receive from you the record layout you are sending.

That's the downstream approach.  The upstream approach for Corner Office 
Idiots emphasises that unless there is an alteration in Team Dynamics and 
Communicative Modalities then it is your opinion as a Professional that 
the Organisation should seriously consider what fallback position should 
be assumed in the event of this project's generating an unsatisfactory 
result.  (fancy talk for 'You'd better be getting Plan B ready')

Folks *hate* getting Plan B ready... and firing off that signal-flare,, 
early and with full documentation down to Taylorite metrics, might cause a 
few other things to open up, like lines of communications between folks 
who actually Do Stuff.

Oh... and as mentioned above, keep your bragsheet polished.

DD
```

#### ↳ Re: Arithmetic Sign In COBOL

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-20T02:24:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<J4qbo.51781$De6.647@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<8gdq669aqm908iaqpt57hpb0t7jhm5g50v@4ax.com>`

```
Having read your note  and the rest of the thread, I (sort-of) agree taht 
you need SOME information about the "other program".  HOWEVER,  you have 
also indicated that:
A) the other program wants the output to have a X"F" sign-nibble
B) that your program never produces a negative output.

THEREFORE, a simple solution is:
   Change TRAN-AMT from
        PIC S9(9)V99.
to
        PIC  9(9)V99.

If there is no "S" in a USAGE DISPLAY output numeric field, then Enterpirse 
COBOL will put a X"F" as the sign-nibble on all output operations.

No other changes should be required in your program or the other program. 
For IBM Enterprise COBOL, usage DISPLAY numeric fields, those two PIC 
clauses require the same storage.  You should be aware, however, (and 
comment in your program) that if this field ever - IN THE FUTURE - needs to 
hold negative values, then you will need to make other changes.

If you have a numeric (usage display) field that you plan on "sharing" with 
another program AND the field can be negative, then the use of SIGN SEPARATE 
is often (almost always) a "good solution".

NOTE: Enterprise COBOL almost always accepts a X"F" as a "valid" input sign 
nibble for numeric fields with an "S" in the picture-claue, but will produce 
X"C" on output.  It is possible that the "other program" is using 
NUMPROC(PFD" - if it is even in COBOL - but that is just a guess.

NOTE2:  This is a "common" problem in migrating from IBM mainframes to 
"ASCII" environments.  IBM mainframes distinguish between unsigned and 
positve USAGE DISPLAY numbers, while ASCII environments (by default - 
without any EBCDIC or IBM compatibility switch) treat these two as 
identical.

"SkippyPB" <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote in message 
news:8gdq669aqm908iaqpt57hpb0t7jhm5g50v@4ax.com...
>
> I thought this issue had gone away with the passing of the IBM-370
…[52 more quoted lines elided]…
> Steve

> the high nibble (4 bits in a byte for the whippersnappers) contain the
> sign on packed fields???

<snip>

Allistair,
  I think you are confused.  The "original" field (the "tran-amt" field) 
that is causing the problem is ZONED decimal, not packed-decimal, i.e. USAGE 
DISPLAY.  For Enterprise COBOL (and all IBM mainframe COBOL's) this is 
stored with the sign-nibble in the high-order nibble of the last byte 
(unless SIGN IS SEPARATE is specified).  This is similar for the LAST BYTE 
to packed-decimal (COMP-3).

The "intermediate" fields are COMP-3, but not the "field in question"  - 
unless I misread the thread.
```

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2010-08-21T13:36:57+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i4okpp$9$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
In article <4krt66tsli6jur3vl6k7i0ukhd7rtsf2f2@4ax.com>,
Clark F Morris  <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
>On Fri, 20 Aug 2010 13:39:18 -0400, SkippyPB
><swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>generated.

I will now admit to joining and/or being beaten with the General Club of 
Confusion.  For the EBCDIC character-ste: as I understand such things a 
field defined as PIC 9(9)V99 should, as a rule, contain only the the 
characters X'F0' - X'F9' (or the 'display numerics').  A field defined as 
S9(9)V99 should, as a rule, contain (left-to-right) for the first ten 
positions the characters X'F0' - X'F9' and in the last (rightmost) 
position a sign-overpunch field interpreted as both the digit value and 
the sign for the entire field (said sign being interpreted as, most often, 
the character 'C' (X'C3') for positive and 'D' (X'C4') for negative.

If these beliefs are reflective of experience then a statement of 'A move 
of unsigned to signed will normally cause a "C" sign to be generated 
whereas the reverse move will cause an "F" sign to be generated' is at 
best partially correct.

An unsigned field is considered to be the same as an usigned number as 
human-being type people see it, ie positive, so given two fields:

01  FLD1 PIC  9V99 VALUE .01.
01  FLD2 PIC S9V99.

... then the code:

INITIALIZE FLD2.
COMPUTE FLD2 = (FLD1 + 0).

... will populate FLD2 with X'F0F0C1'.  Notice the overpunch 
sign-character in the high-order nibble of the low-order byte.

The reverse of this would be accomplished by:

INITIALIZE FLD1.
MOVE FLD2 TO FLD1.

... and this, I believe, will populate FLD1 with X'F0F0F1'.  Notice that 
while the high-order nibble of the low-order byte is an X'F' this *is not 
and cannot be considered a sign* any more than a field defined as

01  FLDA PIC X VALUE '0'.

... can be considered as containing a sign... or so I was taught, lo, 
those many years ago.

Has something changed in the interim?

DD
```

tell us/me) what NUMPROC compiler option you (not the other program) is 
using?  As far as I know, ALL the numproc compiler options are supposed to 
CREATE valid sign-nibbles on output; it is for input that the values that 
are accepted vary.

I could be mistaken, but if you are using an unsigned (zoned decimal) output 
numeric field and you get a X"C" in the sign-nibble, I think this is 
"aparable".
```

I took the S off of the PIC and moved the packed 3 field to it.  When
I browsed the output file, I saw that the PIC 9(9)V99 field looked
just like it did when it was PIC S9(9)V99.

As a point of reference, if you will, I solved the issue by defining a
working storage field as PIC 9(9)V99 and then redefined it as PIC
9(11).  I then redefined the TRAN-AMT field (which was defined as PIC
S9(9)V99) as PIC X(11).  The moved the PIC 9(11) field to the PIC
X(11) field.  A browse of the file showed that the last byte sign
field was now an F.

  UNDER NO CIRCUMSTANCES

  Pic 9(n)v99
then this isn't an issue (for them), but they do not know of any way to get

a bug in their compiler.

X"F" is epected on output for all values of the NUMPROC and OPTcompiler

#### ↳ Re: Arithmetic Sign In COBOL

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2010-08-20T13:08:21-03:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5l9t665s0i6akpif5uvcdopl0g2sgaeup6@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<8gdq669aqm908iaqpt57hpb0t7jhm5g50v@4ax.com>`

```
On Thu, 19 Aug 2010 10:03:18 -0400, SkippyPB
<swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:

>
>I thought this issue had gone away with the passing of the IBM-370
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
>This is IBM mainframe, IBM Enterprise COBOL for Z/OS 4.2.0.

A number of things come to mind.  The first is that I would be
uncomfortable with intermediate fields in calculations being defined
as unsigned.  The second is that the NUMPROC compile option defines
whether a field defined as S9something must have a sign nibble of 'C'
positive or 'D' negative as opposed to having a sign nibble of 'F' -
unsigned to be treated as positive.  The options as I recall are PFD =
must be signed, NOPFD - which has interesting performance implication,
and MIG - migrate which accepts both.  If any of the programs are
compiled using code generated by CSP (cross system product) a field
defined as signed will be unsigned or negative.  
>
>Regards,
```

<snip>

>You forgot the sign of E (can't remember if it was positive or not but
> think it was negative). Call it an undocumented feature.

For IBM's Enterprise COBOL, the use of X"A" thru X"F" (i.e. include "A" "B" 
and "E") in sign-nibbles relates to the NUMCLS (not NUMPROC) installation 
option.  See:

http://publibfp.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr/BOOKS/igy3cg50/2.44

## Thread: OT: What Would You Do In This Situation? — 29 this month / 30 total

### OT: What Would You Do In This Situation?

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2010-08-19T18:37:12-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<q8CdncXzgbuKL_DRnZ2dnUVZ_qydnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
I am looking for more concrete options on how to handle the following 
situation.

I have been given an existing, "small" system developed by a contractor 
using MS Access 2003 and Visual Basic for Applications (VBA).  The 
contractor is gone and there is no way to talk to him.  There is no 
documentation  or requirements just a few screen prints. Before he left he 
offered to come back and fix any problems, but he was told that they would 
not be asking him back under any circumstances.  Although the system was 
"almost ready" fo a long time, it seemed never to get completed.  Parts of 
the application work but other parts do not.  I do not know if it was 
accidentily broken or if it was sabotaged.  The person who was the project 
leader at the time seems to think it was broken on purpose.  The person who 
first got stuck with this project gave me some files saying "this is what I 
think is the latest copy of things.  He wasn't too big on documenting things 
and was terribly disorganized and saved everything so you will find multiple 
versions of everything but no real help on which was the latest stuff".

The system is a COBOL Analysis and Rule Extraction System, but that title is 
misleading.  It is actually an aid to application modernization and there is 
data on programs, databases, database keys, databse layouts, CICS 
transaction, screens and fields, JCL, external databases etc.  I think the 
part that used to work but is now broken is related to parsing the COBOL 
source code.

My first response was:" I do not know MS Access or Visual Basic". They said 
they realized that there would be a significant learning curve, however I am 
not certain that their expectations are realistic.  I asked the person who 
gave me the files how long she thought it would take to learn Access and 
VBA. She said that since the VBA code is pretty advanced she would say it 
would take about one year.  When I mentioned this to my project leader he 
said he was certain I could learn it faster than that.   I am not sure why I 
was picked for this, but in one of the emails the former project leader 
said: "if anybody can do ir Charlie H. can".  He is now retired so he gets 
to go on living :-).

So I am three quarters of the way through the first book, teaching myself 
Access 2003.  I have three other books on VBA.  Psychologically the 
beginning of a project is the hardest time for me.  I am uncomfortable until 
I get some feel for the scope and extent of a project and some idea of how I 
am going to solve the problems.  On this project that uncomfortable period 
is greatly extended as I take the time to learn the tools I need so I can 
analyze the state of the project.

Today, in an attempt to try and relieve some of my anxiety about this 
project I decided to look at some of the files that I have been given and 
see how much I could glean with the current state of my knowledge.  For each 
COBOL program there is one Access 2003  database.  Since there are 9000 to 
10,000 COBOL programs there are a lot of these databases, but they seem 
relatively sraightforward.

There are 10 other files, the ones that are "probably the latest versions". 
Some of these are on the LAN so I could not see them as my LAN access is 
currently broken.  One of these is a data dictionary Access database.  It 
contains 92 tables plus plenty of forms, reports, queries, macros and VBA 
code.  Several of the other files contain 15 to 20 tables with fewer forms, 
reports and queries but a lot of VBA code.  This is not my idea of a small 
application.  Needless to say, rather than decreasing my anxiety it was very 
greatly increased.

I could retire now if I have to, and other factors have had me leaning in 
that direction before this, but I would like to keep working at least until 
January 2013.

I could keep on learning and postpone the decision to retire until later.

I could write an email reporting my concerns from my initial findings and 
see what kind of response I get and obtain a better feeling for management's 
expectations.  Not having a good of idea of what is expected and in what 
timeframe it is expected is contibuting to my anxiety.

I could postpone reporting my concerns until I have learned more about 
Access 2003 and VBA.  Greater knowledge might result in reduced concern on 
my part although I doubt it.

I would greatly appeciate any concrete advice that would help me expand my 
options.  Thanks in advance for you help.
```

#### ↳ Re: OT: What Would You Do In This Situation?

- **From:** e p chandler <epc8@juno.com>
- **Date:** 2010-08-19T18:10:55-07:00

- **Message-ID:** `<d7681aa2-ab8f-4d00-ba26-43fe88cc5b62@f42g2000yqn.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Aug 19, 6:37 pm, "Charles Hottel" <chot...@earthlink.net> wrote:
> I am looking for more concrete options on how to handle the following
> situation.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> contractor is gone and there is no way to talk to him.

[snip]

> I could retire now if I have to, and other factors have had me leaning in
> that direction before this, but I would like to keep working at least until
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> options.  Thanks in advance for you help.

I work with VBA much more than I do with COBOL. VBA itself is not a
difficult language. What is hard to work with is dealing with the many
objects and methods that VBA can take advantage of when a particular
external library is referred to in a project. (Even though VBA is not
really "object oriented".)

This is compounded somwhat by VBA being a product of Microsoft. There
are multple ways to accomplish the same thing, each of which seems to
require an obscure string of commands or parameters that are buried
somewhere in the vast library that is MSDN. (I'm being a *bit*
sarcastic. Actually Microsoft's documentation is not *that* bad.)

For example, you can link to a database using low level OLE or one of
its wrappers like DAO or ADO. You can connect to a database using a
driver or ODBC. You can use SQL.

The other part of the equation is working through the recordset that
queries return.

Etc.

--- e
```

#### ↳ Re: What Would You Do In This Situation?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-08-20T14:22:02+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
